    {
        "body": "View this post on the web at https://gammaradio.substack.com/p/cyber-job-hunting-aint-easy\r\n\r\nThis was a first for me.\r\nI saw two interesting cyber detection jobs that would allow me back into the “hands-on keyboard” world that I love so much. I was excited to apply, took the “Doctor” and “Senior” off my resume, and fired off an application to their respective applicant tracking systems (ATS). After uploading my resume, I then had to retype the entire thing into various fields and reformat for visibility.  Evidently, the AI they are using isn’t that smart yet.  I answered the questions and hit submit, feeling good about my prospects.\r\nThanks for reading Adrian’s Substack! Subscribe for free to receive new posts and support my work.\r\nWithin hours, I received replies from each company.  At first, I thought, “Wow! That was fast for the follow-up gatekeeper questions.”  But my excitement was quickly deflated.  Both emails said, “Thanks, but no thanks, we’ve looked at your resume and don’t think you can do what we need.” \r\nI’ve received many such messages over the past six months.  I know that I will receive more after October 4th, but these two hurt me to my core. Ouch! \r\nI have held my Doctorate in Cybersecurity for one year and a Master’s in the same field for eight years. I’ve held many jobs in the networking and cybersecurity fields and have broad knowledge across nearly every side of the discipline, down to clearing registers in assembly language.  I always say that my knowledge is “a mile wide and an inch deep,” but that sells me short.  My exposure to most sides of networking and cybersecurity is vast. I say this not to brag, but to say that if I am not an expert in a specific application or concept, I can most likely get up to speed fairly quickly.  For example, I have always mentored young IT networking professionals to learn the basics of networking, routing, and switching. Once they know these core disciplines,  it will not be hard for them to pick up and configure almost any networking device. \r\nDespite this, it feels nearly impossible for an experienced cyber subject matter expert (SME) to pivot from one role in cybersecurity to another. The ATS filters us out, the hiring manager doesn’t find the exact keyword in our resume, or an interviewer passes us over because we forgot to name a specific step in the Intelligence Cycle, even if we’re intimately familiar with it. I know this is common practice as I’ve been on government hiring boards and was told to mark people down if they did not mention a specific word or phrase  \r\nYour certifications, degrees, or experience mean nothing if one key word is missing or experience with a specific application is missing from your resume. \r\nIs this fair?  No. But this is the job market that thousands of us are facing, and it is soul-crushing. \r\nThe Wild West of the Early Internet\r\nThinking back on my own skills….I started an Internet Service Provider (ISP) business back in the 90s, learned networking and Linux on my own, and dove headfirst into the world of selling Internet access. I wrote a LinkedIn article five years [ https://link.sbstck.com/redirect/3eeaf8ca-8850-45e0-a36e-c8393243caa9?j=eyJ1IjoiM2Nvd3cyIn0.gBvAYq-JWuxryCpqSAq7s4DN-5PW_O4Y8XoOyZ09efo ] ago that I just remembered, which presents details that I don’t mention below.  To be honest, I forgot that I wrote that article. I may have gotten things mixed up between the two, so don’t be surprised if there are slight discrepancies.\r\nFortunately, archive.org [ https://link.sbstck.com/redirect/75cb1a50-ca89-4c05-b145-51ba7fe25597?j=eyJ1IjoiM2Nvd3cyIn0.gBvAYq-JWuxryCpqSAq7s4DN-5PW_O4Y8XoOyZ09efo ] has an evolution of our home page from those days.  Here is one of the first. \r\nFrom Modems to Missiles\r\nI had no idea how to wire up an RJ-45 connector using the T-568B spec [ https://link.sbstck.com/redirect/e3948eac-b082-4305-bc8e-1ffaa9d0a4de?j=eyJ1IjoiM2Nvd3cyIn0.gBvAYq-JWuxryCpqSAq7s4DN-5PW_O4Y8XoOyZ09efo ]  (orange/white, orange, green/white, blue, blue/white, green, brown/white, brown).  It did not matter at first because 100BaseT was still very popular.  I piled up O’Reilly books and learned so much from my ISP days. My first Linux distro was Yggdrasil [ https://link.sbstck.com/redirect/7a0abfb3-0c0f-41df-b6ce-74efeb5b9850?j=eyJ1IjoiM2Nvd3cyIn0.gBvAYq-JWuxryCpqSAq7s4DN-5PW_O4Y8XoOyZ09efo ] with kernel version 0.98.  We had to compile the kernel ourselves after making a few changes in the config.h file before running make.\r\nIt was so much fun back in those early days.  I had a stack of sixteen, 33.6Kbps Supra modems that we purchased from who knows where. We bought a Livingston Router and Portmaster serial/modem appliance, a switch, and an Adtran DSU, and I learned RADIUS, DNS, Sendmail, USENET, and POP3 on my own. I am sure many of my readers can recall those days.  \r\nI’ll point out RADIUS to  my #infosec readers.  Let me know if you see anything off about a typical user’s setup in the RADIUS users file.\r\nkelly   Cleartext-Password := “mypassword”\r\n            Service-Type = Framed-User,\r\n            Framed-Protocol = PPP\r\nYes, we asked our customers to give us their passwords, which we also used for their POP3 email accounts.  We stored them unprotected in the users file.  This was the wild west.\r\nI set up an NCSA-HTTPd web server and leased a T1 line that terminated in my parents’ unfinished basement in rural Virginia, probably one of the first installations of its kind in Virginia. Centel (Sprint) was our last-mile provider, and we used MCI for Internet connectivity. I recall pricing was around $2,400 for a T1 (1.544 Mbps). I realized fairly quickly that our stack of 16 modems would never need a full T1, so we switched to a fractional T1. But we learned the hard way.\r\nI discovered our first system hack when someone leveraged a WU-FTP server vulnerability, got into my Sendmail server, snagged usernames, and started sending emails to all of my customers! The local police department had never seen such a case of computer crime (this was probably around 1997). Nothing ever came of it, but I learned about “patching” and keeping up with the latest security releases of all of our applications manually. I cannot remember the mastermind’s name, but I do remember seeing them several years later when they were a student at state university. In other words, it was a local kid.  \r\nI remember attending ISPCon (Baltimore 1998 [ https://link.sbstck.com/redirect/d70dacd4-d110-4e55-bb80-f6a2a5154619?j=eyJ1IjoiM2Nvd3cyIn0.gBvAYq-JWuxryCpqSAq7s4DN-5PW_O4Y8XoOyZ09efo ]) and seeing all of the goodies being sold to ISP owners like me. And, there was SWAG back then. I still have my ISPCon bag from Baltimore. One way to describe ISPCon is that it could be thought of as an ancestor of today’s Blackhat conference. It was an evolution from the world of the dial-up BBS server to TCP/IP Internet access, and very exciting for us geeks.\r\nIf I recall correctly, ISPCon was the first place that I saw the Carrier Class US Robotics Total Control modem appliance.  It was rack-mounted with T1/PRI ISDN cards and numerous 56K modems and ISDN cards.  It was a glorious piece of hardware. \r\nWe ended up buying two for our ISP when our rack of 48 or so external dial-up modems became too hard to handle.  Imagine a rack with numerous dial-up modems on each shelf, with phone lines and serial cables running to each modem, with each modem screaming and sounding off connections from dial-up customers. We could tell from the tones whether they got a 56Kbps connection (highly desired) or not.  \r\nThe rack was a carpeted, horrendous mess; the room was hot and not designed for racks.  I remember the Movin’ Cool unit we bought that would shoot cool air into the room after pulling the moisture out of the air.  In the beginning, we had to empty its water reservoirs several times a day. One day, my youngest child walked right into that server room and pushed the power button on my Sendmail/POP server. I remember spending hours cleaning it up and restoring it to operation.  Routine back-ups were not common in my shop, so you can imagine my consternation when it was down and customers called in about their Eudora [ https://link.sbstck.com/redirect/36a792cd-c050-4e93-9349-3fbfbe361f83?j=eyJ1IjoiM2Nvd3cyIn0.gBvAYq-JWuxryCpqSAq7s4DN-5PW_O4Y8XoOyZ09efo ] mail not working. By then, we had a thousand customers and maybe two T1s worth of phone lines (24 lines per T1) coming into the server room.\r\nIf I recall correctly, while we were in talks to sell our business, Cornerstone Networks (CStone) helped us buy a brand new US Robotics Total Control system, or at least add one more to our inventory.  I remember unpacking this Total Control Hub.  It was awesome and really cleaned things up, allowing remote administration access using a GUI and digital ISDN modems.  To me, it was like owning a Corvette, complete with the high cost. A fully loaded Total Control hub could easily cost over $60,000. \r\nI loved working for myself, but I was a tough boss and put in many long hours while still working a full-time job writing software for the Tomahawk Missile Launch Control System [ https://link.sbstck.com/redirect/40d31d7f-16d7-4cd7-aa23-e9212bf71308?j=eyJ1IjoiM2Nvd3cyIn0.gBvAYq-JWuxryCpqSAq7s4DN-5PW_O4Y8XoOyZ09efo ]. I can still remember performing tech support from our townhouse in Fredericksburg, Virginia. Helping customers recall their passwords was a popular problem to deal with. Other problems were getting the fastest download speeds possible.  I cannot count how many times customers complained of being disconnected or slow download speeds.\r\nOne of the tweaks at the time was to have the user set up their modem to use COM2 port to avoid conflict with other onboard devices. This involved moving a jumper on the actual modem card or flipping a DIP switch. Some settings could be adjusted on the modem by using AT commands, like flow control, compression, and serial port speed.  We tried to squeeze all that we could out of our dial-up modems back then.\r\nBefore Microsoft Windows had support for Dial-Up Networking, we distributed a Netscape web browser and Trumpet Winsock on a floppy disk to new customers. Trumpet Winsock was used as the TCP/IP stack until Windows 95 brought a native dialer.  I remember the fanfare when Microsoft introduced the Internet Explorer Administration Kit (IEAK) to automate the setting up and customization of the Internet Explorer web browser, which they developed to compete with Netscape.\r\nAnyhow, I sold that ISP when it became too much to handle, with over 1,000 dial-up customers and me being the only technical person around. Before the Internet bubble popped, I worked for the ISP that bought mine, CStone, out of Charlottesville, and their eventual owner, nTelos [ https://link.sbstck.com/redirect/0c4c0f2c-37ef-4197-9c9d-5444199e58d4?j=eyJ1IjoiM2Nvd3cyIn0.gBvAYq-JWuxryCpqSAq7s4DN-5PW_O4Y8XoOyZ09efo ].  CStone was a start-up business run by three super-smart guys, one of whom was a fellow Hokie [ https://link.sbstck.com/redirect/288a9ccd-19e9-4843-930a-617524d509dd?j=eyJ1IjoiM2Nvd3cyIn0.gBvAYq-JWuxryCpqSAq7s4DN-5PW_O4Y8XoOyZ09efo ], just off the downtown open-air mall.  We had some sharp folks there, including a system administrator who taught me a lot about FreeBSD and the concept of hardening servers from attack. After CStone bought my business, if I remember correctly, they bought another ISP or two before their eventual sale.  These were fun times!\r\nI was a lead engineer when Cornerstone bought its first 1 TB server for our USENET feed storage. Can you imagine paying $30K for 1 TB worth of hard disks?  I recall the total cost being somewhere around that number and probably barely enough to contain all the pictures and binaries that were sent around on USENET at the time.\r\nWith Ntelos, an awesome company at the time, I learned even more and became the lead IP engineer over their multi-state network. I learned Border Gateway Protocol [ https://link.sbstck.com/redirect/39a2c62c-bd76-4a6e-b9ad-4f2c2e2e5089?j=eyJ1IjoiM2Nvd3cyIn0.gBvAYq-JWuxryCpqSAq7s4DN-5PW_O4Y8XoOyZ09efo ] (BGP) on the job, trying to balance the Internet load (numerous DS3s, which were 45 Mbps or so). I took a Cisco class at the local community college and received my Cisco Certified Networking Associate (CCNA) certification. I was a big shot…in my own head!\r\nNtelos continued buying ISPs, and we continued integrating them into our network.  Also,  competitive local exchange carriers [ https://link.sbstck.com/redirect/315bcc19-dea5-4046-b016-583e3ab4aea2?j=eyJ1IjoiM2Nvd3cyIn0.gBvAYq-JWuxryCpqSAq7s4DN-5PW_O4Y8XoOyZ09efo ] (CLECs) became profitable at this time.  Why buy your modem phone lines from the local phone company when you can operate your own phone company? In Virginia, one had to get permission from the State Corporation Commission and probably the FCC to operate as a CLEC. This was not a cheap or easy process. I remember traveling down to Abington, in southwest Virginia, as Ntelos looked at integrating one of the ISPs turned CLECs in the region.  My competitor in Halifax, Virginia, owned by a UVA graduate, was smart enough to convert their ISP business into a CLEC themselves instead of selling out. I believe they are still in operation today.  Despite being me being a Hokie, I’m proud of them. They, too, proved that rural Virginia has some smart people.\r\nThere is a part two here. I got the entrepreneur itch and left another perfectly good job to start an ISP that offered wireless broadband service in Halifax, Virginia.  That’s another story.\r\nWhy Experience Still Matters\r\nI’ve never had enough time during an interview or room on my resume to show off my technical acumen. And despite not knowing the exact application that an organization is using,  one specific step in the Intelligence Cycle, or not worked with cloud infrastructure for three year, I can probably pick up whatever technology is being used. There are many more like me out there.\r\nPlease hire us.  \r\nWe can still get it done.\r\nThanks for reading Adrian’s Substack! Subscribe for free to receive new posts and support my work.\r\n\r\nUnsubscribe https://substack.com/redirect/2/eyJlIjoiaHR0cHM6Ly9nYW1tYXJhZGlvLnN1YnN0YWNrLmNvbS9hY3Rpb24vZGlzYWJsZV9lbWFpbD90b2tlbj1leUoxYzJWeVgybGtJam95TURJM01UWXlPVEFzSW5CdmMzUmZhV1FpT2pFM05EazJNamsxTml3aWFXRjBJam94TnpVNU1qZ3dORGMyTENKbGVIQWlPakUzT1RBNE1UWTBOellzSW1semN5STZJbkIxWWkwMk1EVXhNRFUzSWl3aWMzVmlJam9pWkdsellXSnNaVjlsYldGcGJDSjkuNVIxZjk0b1Q5Y3RyRWxlbFdwWlBxelplOTJYd24zYkNIVHhlOVV4NlQ0USIsInAiOjE3NDk2Mjk1NiwicyI6NjA1MTA1NywiZiI6ZmFsc2UsInUiOjIwMjcxNjI5MCwiaWF0IjoxNzU5MjgwNDc2LCJleHAiOjIwNzQ4NTY0NzYsImlzcyI6InB1Yi0wIiwic3ViIjoibGluay1yZWRpcmVjdCJ9.sVVk13LQb33PuyJrTCfgyrlcMkHOjMCZBLBzyaexG8o?",

economic troubles, NATO’s continued military aid, and Ukraine’s resilience as key factors enabling such a victory, a notable shift from his prior position that land concessions might be necessary to end the war.\r\nPresident Trump conceded that his personal relationship with Vladimir Putin has not helped resolve the Ukraine war, admitting he once believed his ties could end the conflict but now acknowledges “those ties mean nothing.”\r\nIn a fiery 57-minute UN General Assembly speech, President Trump mocked the UN as ineffective, blasted European allies over immigration and green energy, called climate change “the greatest con job ever,” and declared “your countries are going to hell,” while touting U.S. prosperity under his leadership; his remarks drew little applause from diplomats, veered into denial of established facts, and emphasized his MAGA agenda over multilateral cooperation, though he did pledge a new international initiative against biological weapons.\r\nWhite House press secretary Karoline Leavitt demanded a UN investigation after an escalator at UN headquarters abruptly stopped as President Trump and First Lady Melania stepped on it, though UN officials said the halt was caused accidentally by a safety mechanism.\r\nU.S. Defense Secretary Pete Hegseth has dissolved the Pentagon’s long-running Defense Advisory Committee on Women in the Services, founded in 1951, accusing it of pushing a “divisive feminist agenda” that undermines combat readiness; critics note the committee’s recommendations have historically shaped key laws and policies for women in the military, while supporters of Hegseth point to its advocacy on issues like reproductive healthcare as evidence of politicization.\r\nA Massachusetts family alleges that ICE agents briefly held their 5-year-old autistic daughter outside their Leominster home to pressure her father, Edward Hip Mejia, into surrendering during an immigration operation; video shows the child surrounded by officers, but DHS strongly denied the claim, calling it a “disgusting smear” and accusing Mejia — a longtime U.S. resident from Guatemala with prior arrests — of abandoning his daughter while fleeing; the girl was later returned safely, and Mejia was detained two days later and is now in ICE custody.\r\nNexstar Media Group announced it will keep “Jimmy Kimmel Live!” off its ABC affiliate stations, joining Sinclair in refusing to air the show even as Disney restores it nationally; both station groups, together representing about 70 affiliates, cited Kimmel’s remarks about Republicans following Charlie Kirk’s killing and stressed the need for “respectful, constructive dialogue,” leaving tens of millions of U.S. viewers without broadcast access while the program remains available on Disney’s streaming platforms.\r\nDisney announced new streaming price hikes starting Oct. 21, raising Disney+ with ads to $11.99 (up $2) and Disney+ Premium to $18.99 (up $3), with bundle prices also increasing across Disney+, Hulu, and ESPN plans; the timing follows backlash and cancellation threats over the company’s suspension of Jimmy Kimmel’s late-night show, though Disney has since reinstated him, and comes as Disney pushes its new ESPN app to drive subscribers toward bundled offerings.\r\nErrol Musk, the 79-year-old father of Elon Musk, has been accused in a New York Times investigation of sexually abusing five of his children and stepchildren since the early 1990s, allegations he strongly denies as “false” and “nonsense”; the report, citing family members, personal correspondence, and past police inquiries, details claims of misconduct dating back to 1993, notes that multiple investigations were opened without charges, and underscores Elon Musk’s long-described estrangement from his father, whom he once characterized as capable of “a carefully thought-out plan of evil.”\r\nWith a Sept. 30 deadline looming to avoid a government shutdown, President Trump abruptly canceled a planned meeting with Democratic leaders Chuck Schumer and Hakeem Jeffries, saying their funding demands were “unserious” and that he would only negotiate if they changed course; Democrats accused him of ducking responsibility, warning Trump would “own the shutdown,” while GOP leaders Mike Johnson and John Thune reportedly urged him to call off the talks after Democrats insisted on preserving Obamacare funding.\r\nTuesday’s special election in Arizona’s 7th Congressional District, where Democrat Adelita Grijalva is heavily favored over Republican Daniel Butierez, is expected to give the House the decisive 218th signature needed on a bipartisan petition to force a vote on releasing the Jeffrey Epstein case files; both candidates pledged to sign, making the contest a turning point in an effort GOP leaders have tried to block, with a potential House vote as early as mid-October, though the measure would still face long odds in the Senate.\r\nA Florida jury found Ryan Wesley Routh guilty on all counts for attempting to assassinate Donald Trump at his West Palm Beach golf course in September 2024, as well as assaulting a Secret Service agent and committing multiple firearms violations; the dramatic trial ended with Routh trying to stab himself in the neck with a pen after the verdict, while prosecutors highlighted his written note admitting intent, and Trump hailed the conviction as a “big moment for justice in America.”\r\nDonald Trump has chosen downtown Miami as the site of his future presidential library, on land owned by Miami-Dade College near the historic Freedom Tower, a landmark tied to Cuban refugee history; the project, expected to include an adjacent hotel, marks the first presidential library paired with such a development, and will move forward pending a unanimous Florida Cabinet vote, after state lawmakers cleared legal hurdles to ensure local officials could not block construction.\r\nGood news:\r\nCitizen scientists in Rhode Island’s Jacob’s Point salt marsh are using makeshift “coffee filter arks” to keep endangered saltmarsh sparrow nests afloat during floods, with early results showing a dramatic reduction in chick         "body": "View this post on the web at https://natesnewsletter.substack.com/p/the-complete-ai-interview-guide-what\r\n\r\nLast month, one of my friends got rejected from a job because of AI.\r\nI wish I could say that was unusual. But it’s not. He took an AI interview that didn’t give him time to actually talk about his 15 years of experience in engineering, constantly interrupting him. \r\nAt the same time, I know multiple HR teams that admit privately they are having trouble telling who to hire because candidates keep cheating at interviews.\r\nNobody is happy, and interviews are at the heart of the problem. \r\nWe're in a hiring arms race where everyone's using the same weapons, and everybody's losing.\r\nI've coached dozens of people through job applications in the age of AI—from new grads to senior directors. I've sat across from C-suite executives planning AI-integrated roles, trying to figure out what skills actually matter anymore. I've spent dozens of hours with HR teams who went from confidently screening candidates to admitting they have no idea who's real and who's reading from a screen. \r\nThe problem is catastrophically multi-sided.\r\nAfter months of watching this disaster unfold from every angle, I did what I should have done earlier: I put together my notes from conversations with everyone, dug into every tool on the market, and started exploring what prompts really worked. \r\nBut beyond the prep and the notes, I was looking for something deeper—what philosophy of hiring and interviewing actually elevates human signal in an age when we’re all drowning in artificial noise?\r\nWhat approach to interviewing actually fights the dehumanizing aspects of AI, but takes full advantage of AI as a prep guide?\r\nThe pattern that emerged isn't what the vendors are selling or what the thought leaders are preaching. The winners aren't those avoiding AI or those using it most cleverly. They're the ones who've figured out that the game has fundamentally changed—and they're playing by new rules.\r\nWhat You'll Learn\r\nFor Candidates:\r\nWhy the new STAR-C interview method beats perfect STAR stories every time\r\nThe hard truth about which tools help and which are fakers\r\n9 real in-depth AI prompts that actually prepare you for modern interviews\r\n4 prompts to help you develop a killer interview prep routine\r\n3 prompts to help you see how interviewers assess you—practice cheat codes!\r\n2 prompts to help you develop the language to talk about your AI usage well\r\nHow to prepare for case studies that highlight YOUR judgement, not AI’s\r\nWhen and where to use AI to win\r\nA handy interview prep sheet you can print out and stick by the desk\r\nFor Hiring Managers:\r\nThe 3-tier AI Fluency Assessment that evaluates what actually matters\r\nHow to design interviews that reward genuine problem-solving over hidden AI assistance\r\nWhy your detection tools don't work and what to do instead\r\nCase studies showing why great metrics can hide terrible hiring\r\nA hiring manager prep sheet with key principles and tips for interviews\r\nMy goal is deeply practical: I want to help unstick the hiring impasse. \r\nI want to help you get a job in this market.\r\nI want to help you interview better if you are hiring. \r\nI want you to find ways to get the signal of human talent out there so people find their best roles, and companies can find talent that’s really passionate to solve problems that matter. \r\nThe game has changed, but my conversations have convinced me that human judgment hasn't become less valuable—it's become the whole game. The question isn't whether you use AI. Each level builds on increasing responsibility, technical expertise, and leadership within the Operational Technology (OT) cybersecurity program. Analysts are responsible for protecting, maintaining, and continuously improving the security of OT environments, ensuring compliance with regulatory and industry standards such as NERC CIP and NIST. Level 1 – Cyber Security Analyst Position Concept: The Level 1 Cyber Security Analyst supports OT cybersecurity maintenance activities, manages cyber asset inventories, assists in the implementation of cybersecurity programs, and ensures security controls are applied consistently across operational systems. Key Responsibilities: Identify and document OT cyber assets; extract and update asset configurations in the database. Install security patches, update antivirus definitions, and perform system backups. Maintain baselines of operational systems; update cybersecurity SharePoint sites for stakeholder engagement. Monitor CVE releases and assess applicability to OT assets. Provide IT-related support for OT systems (e.g., HMIs, network appliances, remote connectivity). Required Knowledge/Skills/Abilities: Basic understanding of industrial automation systems (ABB, Allen Bradley, Emerson, etc.) or operating system security (Windows, Linux/Unix, Mac OS). Familiarity with endpoint, server, and network security tools such as Firewalls, IDS/IPS, SIEM, Antivirus, and DLP. Knowledge of forensic investigation techniques for logs, networks, and systems. Working knowledge of networking protocols and system administration. Ability to recognize and investigate indicators of compromise. Strong analytical, communication, and risk assessment skills. Preferred Skills: Experience applying patches, antivirus updates, and performing backups. Experience troubleshooting network communications. Supporting compliance audits. Designing/implementing industrial automation or network architecture. Level 2 – Senior Cyber Security Analyst Position Concept: The Level 2 Analyst is responsible for assessing OT vulnerabilities, ensuring secure configurations, and developing remediation plans. The role includes mentoring junior analysts, developing cybersecurity maintenance procedures, and providing guidance for program compliance. Key Responsibilities: Evaluate OT network architecture, system configurations, and external connections for compliance. Review vendor manuals, training materials, and industry standards to develop cybersecurity procedures. Mentor and provide guidance to Level 1 Analysts. Monitor and assess new CVEs for applicability to OT systems. Support incident response, security intelligence monitoring, and OT awareness initiatives. Required Knowledge/Skills/Abilities: Advanced knowledge of OS security (Windows, Linux/Unix, Mac OS), web servers, and network security. Hands-on experience with security tools: Firewalls, IDS/IPS, SIEM, Endpoint Security, File Integrity Monitoring, etc. Proficiency in forensic investigation (logs, networks, systems). Strong knowledge of scripting (Python, Bash, PowerShell). Familiarity with penetration testing tools and techniques. Understanding of compliance frameworks (NERC CIP, SOX, PCI). Strong communication, risk assessment, and analytical skills. Level 3 – Lead Cyber Security Analyst Position Concept: The Lead Analyst is responsible for maintaining and improving OT cybersecurity frameworks, overseeing compliance activities, leading vulnerability management efforts, and ensuring alignment with industry standards. This role leads cross-departmental collaboration, trains staff and provides subject matter expertise for audits and regulatory engagements. Key Responsibilities: Translate standards (NERC CIP, NIST CSF, NIST 800-53) into systemic and procedural solutions. Maintain and improve OT cybersecurity standards, procedures, and maintenance schedules. Lead vulnerability management and remediation programs. Evaluate OT systems for compliance with regulatory and internal standards. Develop management responses for internal/external audits. Facilitate IT/OT collaboration and train staff on cybersecurity best practices. Required Knowledge/Skills/Abilities: Expertise in developing and implementing OT cybersecurity programs. Strong knowledge of OS, web, and network security across enterprise and OT environments. Advanced expertise in security tools (Firewalls, IDS/IPS, SIEM, DLP, Endpoint Security, etc.). Deep knowledge of forensic investigation, malware analysis, and packet analysis. Proficiency in scripting (Python, Bash, PowerShell). Strong knowledge of compliance frameworks (NERC CIP, SOX, PCI). Excellent leadership, communication, and risk management skills. Preferred Skills: Advanced expertise in reverse engineering malware. Extensive experience integrating compliance controls into OT systems. Education Required (All Levels): High School Diploma or equivalent. Preferred: Bachelor’s degree in computer science, Information Systems, Cybersecurity, or related field. Certifications Required (All Levels): At least one security-related certification (from vendors such as Cisco, (ISC)², GIAC, ISA, ISACA, CompTIA, EC-Council) or ability to obtain within one year of hire. Preferred: Three or more advanced certifications (e.g., CISSP, CISM, CISA, CEH, CCNP, OSCP, GIAC certifications). Experience Level 1: 5+ years related experience in Cybersecurity, IT, OT, industrial automation, or technical fields. May substitute: 2 years with Associate’s Degree or 1 year with Bachelor’s Degree. Level 2: 6+ years related experience. May substitute: 4 years with associate’s degree or 3 years with bachelor’s degree. Level 3: 8+ years related experience.
